J	Pacelle, RL				Pacelle, Richard L., Jr.			The Solicitor General and the United States Supreme Court: Executive Branch Influence and Judicial Decisions	JOURNAL OF POLITICS			English	Book Review							10TH JUSTICE	This article develops and tests a model of conditional status quo bias and American inequality. We find that institutional features that bias policy outcomes toward the status quo have played a central role in the path of inequality. Using time-series analysis of top income shares during the post-Depression period, we identify the Senate as a key actor in the politics of income inequality. Our findings suggest that the supermajoritarian nature of the Senate and policy stagnation, when coupled with economic and social factors that produce rising inequality, create a situation in which inequality becomes difficult to reverse. J	Soifer, HD				Soifer, Hillel David			State-Building and Tax Regimes in Central America	JOURNAL OF POLITICS			English	Book Review								Despite the wide range of studies focused on the causes of terrorism, most use the state as the unit of analysis. Doing so, however, overlooks important variation that occurs within the state. Our research seeks to determine the causes of domestic terrorism through a more refined unit of analysis. We do this by using the PRIO-GRID cell structure spatially merged with a geocoded version of the GTD dataset. We then perform a Getis-Ord Gi* hot spot analysis to uncover those local areas most prone to domestic terrorism. Our results indicate the following attributes increase the likelihood of terrorism: mountainous terrain, close proximity to a state capital, large population, high population density, and poor economic conditions. When testing between regime types, we find that factors such as population, economic conditions, and the number of ethnic groups are significant only in democracies, while distance to capital is significant only in autocracies. J	Stark, A				Stark, Andrew			The Consensus School, Its Critics, and Welfare Policy: A Study of American Political Discourse	JOURNAL OF POLITICS			English	Article							STATE	In the debate between the "consensus" school of American politics and its various critics, all participants have shown themselves capable of shedding light on different historical periods or particular political conflicts; and, of course, given the complexity of public discourse, many American political controversies lend themselves to elucidation by more than one of them. This paper's purpose is to suggest yet a further way of interpreting American political discourse, one that carries the capacity to illuminate a kind of discursive structure in American politics that goes undiscerned by both the consensus school and its critics. The paper illustrates this alternative approach by using it to interpret discourse leading up to and following the 1996 welfare reforms. It concludes by suggesting that since welfare debate lends itself well to this interpretation, the question of whether other American political debates might also do so is a worthwhile project for further research.	Univ Toronto, Toronto, ON M5S 3E6, Canada	University of Toronto	Stark, A (corresponding author), Univ Toronto, Toronto, ON M5S 3E6, Canada.
